# Chapter 1 — Meaning

**Registry 117 — Peace** · *Word Characteristic Summary*

---

## What it is

> Peace is the condition of wholeness, right relationship, and rest — the Hebrew shalom covering far more than the absence of conflict. It means completeness, prosperity, welfare, the flourishing of everything and everyone in their proper place. It is used of the end of hostilities, of the quality of a life well-lived, of the relationship between a person and God when that relationship is restored. The New Testament declares that Christ himself is our peace: through him the fundamental rupture between humanity and God is healed. Peace is the signature of the kingdom.


**Working description:** The negative definition is richly evidenced: (1) Peace is not the absence of war alone — the shalom vocabulary covers inner states that have nothing to do with military conflict (Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8, Lam 3:17); (2) Peace is not self-generated security — the false shalom pattern (2509-002, Deu 29:19) shows that self-claimed peace without covenant ground is its precise corruption; (3) Peace is not available to the wicked — Isa 48:22, 57:21 ("there is no peace for the wicked"); the moral character of the person determines whether shalom is accessible; (4) Peace is not silence or passivity — the silence vocabulary (H2790B, H2814, G4623) is distinct from shalom; silence is a condition that enables or expresses peace but is not peace itself; (5) Peace is not complacent ease — sha'anan (H7600 group 413-001) names self-generated ease as a vice (Amo 6:1: "woe to those who are at ease in Zion"), demonstrating that ease without divine ground is not shalom. The negative boundary: peace is not a condition the person achieves by themselves, claims without God's ground, or inhabits while in moral disorder.


## Name and naming

**T1.1.1 — What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?**

The primary Hebrew name is shalom (sha.lom, H7965G and sub-forms) — the most semantically wide term in the OT inner-being vocabulary. Its breadth is encoded in the STEP gloss: completeness, soundness, welfare, peace, quiet, tranquillity, contentment, friendship, covenant peace, peace from war (OBS-117-009). The name itself reveals that peace is not a simple state but a structural condition of completeness — everything in its proper place. Secondary Hebrew names: nachat (na.chat — quietness/quietude, H5183A), shaqat (sha.qat — to quiet/be quiet, H8252), nuach (nu.ach — rest, H5117), menuchah (me.nu.chah — resting place, H4496H). The primary Greek name is eirēnē (G1515) — which in the LXX became the translation word for shalom, absorbing its full range. The sub-names (YHWH-Shalom: "LORD is peace"; Prince of Peace: sar shalom) reveal that peace is not merely a human condition but a divine identity: the name carries what the named one IS.

**T1.1.2 — What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?**

Strong continuity. The LXX translates shalom with eirēnē throughout the Hebrew Bible, establishing the semantic correspondence as the foundational datum of the NT peace vocabulary. The NT eirēnē carries the full shalom range: (1) Inner felt state (Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God... will guard your hearts and your minds," corresponding to shalom 2508-001); (2) Covenantal gift (Rom 5:1 — "peace with God through our Lord Jesus Christ," corresponding to 2508-002); (3) Moral orientation (Eirēneuō, G1514 — "live peaceably with all," corresponding to 2508-003); (4) Messianic embodiment (Eph 2:14 — "he himself is our peace," corresponding to 2508-004). The correspondence is not merely semantic but structural — the NT's peace vocabulary is the OT's shalom vocabulary transposed into the key of Christ. No significant discontinuity; the NT deepens rather than revises.


## Kind

**T1.2.1 — What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else?**

The core inner-being essence of shalom is integrative wholeness — the condition of the whole person (body, soul, mind, relationships, and orientation toward God) in which everything is in its proper place and functioning rightly. This is not a narrow emotional state but an existential condition. The data establishes this through convergence: (1) The etymology of shalom (completeness, soundness — OBS-117-009) names the absence of fragmentation or deficiency; (2) Lam 3:17 (OBS-117-144) — "my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is" — shows that shalom-deprivation is total inner destitution, not merely sadness; (3) Jer 29:11 (OBS-117-149) — "welfare... future... hope" — the three outcomes of God's sha.lom-intention show peace as the condition that makes flourishing possible; (4) Isa 26:3 (OBS-117-144) — perfect peace for the mind stayed on God — shows that shalom is the integrative outcome of right orientation. The essence is best stated: peace is the condition of integrative wholeness in which the person is complete, secure, and in right relation — capable of their full creaturely purpose.

**T1.2.2 — Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?**

All four, in structured combination: (1) State: shalom as the inner condition of the person (2508-001 — inner peace as felt experience; 2509-001 — welfare as existential condition); (2) Act: shalom as active orientation and pursuit (2508-003 — "I am for peace"; Psa 34:14 — "seek peace and pursue it"; G1514 eirēneuō — actively being at peace with others); (3) Quality: shalom as the stable character quality of the peaceable person (Mal 2:6 — walked in peace and uprightness; G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as quality of heavenly wisdom; G2272 — gentle and quiet spirit as character); (4) Relationship: shalom as the condition of the God-human relationship (2508-002 — covenant peace; G1517 — making peace through the blood of Christ; Rom 5:1 — peace with God). The evidence does not privilege any one of these over the others — they are structurally interdependent. The relational dimension (covenant) is the ground; the state dimension (felt condition) is the fruit; the quality dimension (character) is the formed expression; the act dimension (pursuit, peacemaking) is the volitional engagement.


## Boundary — what it is not

**T1.3.1 — What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?**

Peace vs. rest (nuach, menuchah): rest is the condition of cessation from striving, the physical and inner stopping; peace is the positive condition of wholeness that rest may produce or accompany. The two overlap heavily (nuach and shalom co-occur throughout the corpus) but are distinguishable: nuach can be refused by God as judgment (Deu 28:65 — no resting-place); shalom may persist even in movement and striving. Peace vs. joy (H7600/G1515 vs. R97): joy is the positive emotional response; peace is the positive existential condition that may produce joy but is distinct from it. Psa 35:27 (the Lord delights in welfare of his servant) — joy is the emotional response to shalom-welfare, not the same thing. Peace vs. trust (R163): trust is the inner act of reliance on God; peace is the inner condition that trust produces (Isa 26:3 — he trusts in you → perfect peace; Psa 4:8 — you alone make me dwell in safety → I lie down in peace). Trust precedes peace; peace is trust's outcome. Gap: the boundary between peace and righteousness (tsedaqah) is particularly important but incompletely addressed in Stage 2a — Isa 32:17 ("the effect of righteousness will be peace") makes peace the fruit of righteousness, but whether they are distinct or overlapping characteristics requires Session D analysis.

**T1.3.2 — What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?**

Yes — and this is among the most analytically significant features of the peace registry. The false-peace pattern (2508-003 and 2509-002) shows that shalom is precisely the characteristic that can be falsely claimed: (1) False prophecy of shalom: Jer 6:14, 8:11, Eze 13:10, 13:16, Mic 3:5 — prophets cry "Peace, peace" when there is no peace (prior session, incorporated as source material); (2) Self-deceptive shalom: Deu 29:19 (2509-002, OBS-117-150) — "I shall be safe, though I walk in the stubbornness of my heart"; (3) Complacent ease (sha'anan, H7600 group 413-001) — the comfortable feel of worldly security mistaken for shalom; (4) Speaking shalom with evil in the heart: Psa 28:3, Jer 9:8 — "speaks peace to his neighbor while evil is in his heart." The implication: shalom is the most counterfeitable of inner-being characteristics because its absence-of-distress phenomenology can be replicated by complacency, self-deception, or false prophecy. Authentic shalom requires its proper ground (God, covenant, trust) while false shalom is the same phenomenological condition without that ground.

**T1.3.3 — What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?**

Yes — the data shows at least four levels: (1) Shalom shalom (Isa 26:3 — perfect/complete peace, OBS-117-144) — the doubled word signals the superlative form, the fullest inner peace available to the trust-directed mind; (2) Great peace (Psa 119:165 — "great peace have those who love your law," OBS-117-144) — abundant but not doubled; (3) Present peace — the ordinary condition of shalom available to the covenant person (Psa 4:8, Num 6:26); (4) Absent peace — the condition of the bereft soul (Lam 3:17, Jer 30:5, Eze 7:25). Additionally: eschatological peace (Isa 9:7 — "no end to his peace") represents a fullness beyond present experience. The data does not articulate a formal scale but the textual evidence implies progression from absent through ordinary through great to perfect. The doubled shalom (Isa 26:3) and the "no end" formulation (Isa 9:7) mark the extreme ends of the spectrum.


## Modes of operation

**T1.4.1 — In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?**

The data shows a rich cognate field. Hebrew near-synonyms functioning alongside shalom: (1) Nuach/menuchah (rest, resting-place) — the cessation-dimension of peace; (2) Nachat (H5183A — quietness) — the inner stillness dimension, co-occurs with shaqat in Isa 30:15 at the analytical summit; (3) Shaqat (H8252 — quiet/quieted) — the active quieting, most prominent in its paired form "quietness and trust" (Isa 30:15); (4) Sha'anan (H7600 — at ease) — the ease dimension, positively (Isa 32:18) and negatively (Amo 6:1); (5) Demamah (H1827 — profound silence) — the threshold silence before divine speech. Greek near-synonyms: eirēnē (G1515) is not a synonym but the translation equivalent; hēsuchia/hēsuchios (G2271/G2272) — quietness, the NT equivalent of the Hebrew silence vocabulary (1Pe 3:4 — gentle and quiet spirit); anesis (G0425) — relief/rest from pressure. The cognate field reveals that peace has an inner-face (the silence/stillness vocabulary) and an outer-face (the welfare/wholeness vocabulary), and shalom is the integrative term that spans both.

**T1.4.2 — Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?**

The data provides explicit antonyms: (1) War (milchamah) — Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace, but when I speak, they are for war"); the most direct antonym in the moral-orientation register; (2) Evil (ra'ah) — Jer 29:11 ("plans for welfare and not for evil"); the antonym in the divine-intention register; (3) Calamity/disaster — Isa 45:7 ("I make well-being and create calamity"); the antonym in the creational register; (4) Terror/panic — Jer 30:5 ("a cry of panic, of terror, and no peace"); the experiential antonym; (5) Stubbornness/iniquity — Deu 29:19, Mal 2:6 ("turned many from iniquity"); moral disorder as peace's contrary; (6) Anguish — Eze 7:25 ("when anguish comes they will seek peace, but there shall be none"). The antonym profile confirms that peace is not a neutral absence of distress but a positive condition with multiple contraries — war (relational), evil (moral), calamity (existential), terror (experiential), stubbornness (dispositional).

**T1.4.3 — Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?**

Yes — three paradoxes are documented: (1) Peace through suffering: Isa 53:5 ("the chastisement that brought us peace, and with his wounds we are healed") — peace is purchased through suffering, not despite it (OBS-117-147). The mechanism of peace is its opposite (pain, punishment); (2) Peace through bitterness: Isa 38:17 ("it was for my welfare [shalom] that I had great bitterness") — Hezekiah's retrospective: his suffering was the path to his welfare. The paradox: shalom-welfare arrived via its experiential opposite; (3) Complacent ease as false peace: H7600 group 413-001 (Amo 6:1) and group 413-002 (Isa 32:18) — the same experiential condition of ease is either vice (self-generated) or blessing (divinely given). Peace and its counterfeit may be phenomenologically indistinguishable. The paradoxes converge on a single insight: authentic shalom is not produced by the absence of suffering or difficulty but by the presence of the peace-giver. Its opposite conditions (suffering, bitterness, difficulty) can be the means by which it arrives.


## Immediate response

**T1.5.1 — What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?**

The immediate responses to shalom's presence documented in the data: (1) Rest and sleep — Psa 4:8 ("in peace I will both lie down and sleep"); shalom immediately enables the cessation of vigilance; (2) Absence of fear — Judg 6:23 ("Peace be to you. Do not fear") — OBS-117-144; the divine peace immediately displaces terror; (3) Gladness and praise — Psa 35:27 ("shout for joy and be glad... Great is the Lord, who delights in the welfare of his servant") — OBS-117-149; shalom's presence triggers praise; (4) Heart-joining — 1Ch 12:17 ("my heart will be joined to you") — OBS-117-151; shalom's presence in friendship produces heart-union; (5) Daniel's strengthening — Dan 10:19 ("peace be with you; be strong and of good courage") — the angelic peace immediately produces inner strength. The pattern: shalom's immediate effect is the displacement of its contraries (fear, vigilance, inner fragmentation) and the enabling of their alternatives (rest, praise, courage, heart-union).

**T1.5.2 — Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?**

Documented immediate responses to shalom's absence: (1) Soul bereft — Lam 3:17 ("my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is") — total inner destitution, loss of access to happiness itself; (2) Bones wasting — Psa 32:3 (prior session: "when I kept silent, my bones wasted away through my groaning all day long") — somatic deterioration; (3) Trembling heart — Deu 28:65 ("a trembling heart and failing eyes and a languishing soul") — the triple somatic-inner expression of peacelessness in judgment; (4) Anguish seeking — Eze 7:25 ("when anguish comes they will seek peace, but there shall be none"); (5) Job's triple negation — Job 3:26 ("I am not at ease, nor am I quiet... I have no rest, but trouble comes"). The withdrawal of shalom is experienced as total inner collapse — not discomfort but destitution. The soul's access to its own capacity for happiness is severed (Lam 3:17).

**T1.5.3 — Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly.**

Yes — the refusal of peace pattern is one of the most analytically significant in the registry. The data shows peace offered and refused: (1) Isa 28:12 ("This is rest, give rest to the weary... but they would not hear") — rest/peace offered and refused by Israel; (2) Jer 6:16 ("stand by the roads, and look, and ask for the ancient paths, where the good way is; and walk in it, and find rest for your souls. But they said, 'We will not walk in it'") — rest explicitly located in the "ancient path" and refused; (3) Isa 30:15 ("In returning and rest you shall be saved; in quietness and in trust shall be your strength. But you were unwilling") — the most concentrated statement of peace refused. The inability to receive peace is not ignorance but refusal — the will actively declines the condition. This pattern is concentrated in the OT prophets and names a structural feature of the human condition: peace is available but resisted.


## Sustained effect

**T1.6.1 — What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?**

The sustained effects of shalom documented in the data: (1) Stumble-proof stability — Psa 119:165 ("great peace have those who love your law; nothing can make them stumble") — sustained shalom produces inner stability resistant to external shocks; (2) Wisdom reception — Isa 32:17 ("the effect of righteousness will be peace, and the result of righteousness, quietness and trust forever") — sustained peace is the condition in which trust is formed and wisdom operates; (3) Turning many from iniquity — Mal 2:6 — Levi's sustained walk in peace produced transformative effects in others; (4) Righteousness harvest — Jam 3:18 ("a harvest of righteousness is sown in peace by those who make peace") — peace-orientation produces righteousness as its long-term fruit; (5) Strength — Isa 30:15 ("in quietness and in trust shall be your strength") — the sustained inner posture of quiet trust produces strength. The sustained profile: shalom is not only an experienced condition but a formative one — it produces stability, trust, strength, and the capacity to impact others.

**T1.6.2 — What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?**

Sustained absence of shalom produces progressive deterioration: (1) Somatic degradation — Psa 38:3 ("no soundness in my flesh... no health in my bones because of my sin") — sustained peacelessness shows in the body; (2) Exile and homelessness — Deu 28:65 ("you shall find no respite, and there shall be no resting place for the sole of your foot") — sustained peacelessness produces the ultimate displacement; (3) Universal desolation — Jer 12:12 ("no flesh has peace") — when shalom is withdrawn from a community, the deprivation becomes total; (4) The wicked's false prosperity — Job 21:9 ("their houses are safe from fear") reveals by contrast: the wicked have external safety but not genuine shalom — the sustained absence of authentic peace produces the counterfeit of complacent ease. The trajectory: inner → somatic → communal → eschatological.

**T1.6.3 — Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?**

Yes — and this is theologically significant. The forming dimension: (1) Mal 2:6 — walking in peace as the character-across-time of the faithful priest; the formed person whose sustained walk is peaceful; (2) G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as the fruit of discipline (Heb 12:11 — "the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained by it"); (3) Gal 5:22 — peace as Spirit-fruit — the Spirit forms peace as a characteristic quality in the believer over time; (4) G2272 hēsuchios — the gentle and quiet spirit (1Pe 3:4) as the "hidden person of the heart" — a formed inner quality precious to God. The evidence confirms: sustained shalom is not merely experienced but formed — it becomes a character quality, an inner orientation, and eventually the "hidden person" of the heart. The transformative dimension connects directly to T5 (formative and developmental).


## Conditions of reception

**T1.7.1 — What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?**

Yes — as established in Q&A-020. The data shows: absent (Lam 3:17 — soul bereft), present (Psa 4:8 — lying down in peace), great (Psa 119:165 — great peace for law-lovers), perfect (Isa 26:3 — shalom shalom for the trust-directed mind), eschatological-unlimited (Isa 9:7 — no end to his peace). The degrees are not always explicitly graduated but the comparative constructions confirm graduation is present: "great peace" (rav shalom) implies more-than-ordinary peace; shalom-shalom (doubled) implies maximal completeness. The NT adds: "peace of God which surpasses all understanding" (Phili 4:7) — the eschatological peace exceeds cognitive categories entirely.

**T1.7.2 — What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?**

The data reveals a causal structure governing the degree of shalom: (1) Orientation of the mind toward God (Isa 26:3 — "you keep him in perfect peace whose mind is stayed on you, because he trusts in you") — the directing of the mind toward God produces perfect peace; (2) Love of God's law (Psa 119:165 — "great peace have those who love your law") — orientation toward God's word produces great peace; (3) Righteous character (Isa 32:17 — "the effect of righteousness will be peace") — the degree of shalom follows from the degree of righteousness; (4) Trust (Psa 4:8, Isa 30:15) — trust is the inner act that enables peace's reception; (5) The absence of stubbornness/self-deception (Deu 29:19, Jer 23:17) — the factors that reduce shalom are the absence of trust and the presence of self-deception. The causal structure: covenant faithfulness → trust → orientation toward God → peace. Each factor contributes; disruption at any point reduces the degree of shalom available.

**T1.7.3 — What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?**

Yes — and the eschatological fullness is among the most extensively documented in the programme's data. Evidence: (1) H7965G group 2508-004 (DHC) — the messianic reign of peace: "of the increase of his government and of peace there will be no end" (Isa 9:7); (2) Eph 2:14 (G1515 426-003) — Christ IS peace, achieved once-for-all through the blood of the cross (Col 1:20); (3) Phili 4:7 — the peace that surpasses all understanding as the eschatological reality experienced in anticipation; (4) The covenant of peace that shall not be removed (Isa 54:10, Eze 37:26) — the eternal character of eschatological shalom; (5) Cosmic peace at the Lamb's seventh seal (Rev 8:1 — sigē, silence in heaven — G4602) — the eschatological threshold of total cosmic shalom. The present attainment of shalom (Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8) is real but partial; the eschatological fullness (Isa 9:7, Eph 2:14, Phili 4:7) is the unrestricted reality toward which the present experience points.


## Dimension classification

**T1.8.1 — What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?**

The most important single finding from T1: peace (shalom/eirēnē) is not primarily an emotional state or an absence of conflict, but an integrative wholeness grounded in divine identity and structured through covenant. The evidence establishes four dimensions that are not alternatives but a sequence: (1) Covenantal ground: shalom exists because God covenants it — the covenant of peace (Isa 54:10) is the basis; (2) Relational gift: God gives shalom to those in covenant (Num 6:26), speaks it to his saints (Psa 85:8), plans it for his people (Jer 29:11); (3) Inner condition: the received shalom becomes the experienced condition of the whole person — perfect peace for the trust-directed mind (Isa 26:3), soul at rest (Psa 4:8), welfare of the body and soul (Psa 38:3, 55:18); (4) Eschatological telos: the peace given and experienced is not complete until it is embodied in a Person — the Prince of Peace (Isa 9:6), he who shall be their peace (Mic 5:5), he who himself is our peace (Eph 2:14). DIM-117-002 (the pending finding about the nature of shalom) is hereby resolved: shalom is all four dimensions simultaneously, as a structured and connected reality. The central analytical finding is the sequence: divine identity → covenant gift → inner condition → eschatological embodiment in a Person. [RESOLVING DIM-117-002]

**T1.8.2 — What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?**

Two genuine gaps: (1) Pre-fall peace: the data does not address what shalom was in the original creation before the fall. The earliest peace references (Gen 15, 26, 28) already presuppose a world needing covenant — the pre-fall condition of shalom is not attested in the vocabulary evidence. This is a structural gap in the lexical approach: the peace vocabulary emerges in a fallen world and is therefore always shaped by covenant-restoration; (2) The relationship between the silence/quietness vocabulary (the "inner face" of peace) and shalom itself: the rest/quiet/silence Hebrew terms (H5117, H4496H, H5183A, H8252, and others) form the large majority of the registry's groups, yet their relationship to shalom is not formally defined in the data — they co-occur and co-occur in the same theological contexts but the data does not explicitly state whether shalom produces silence/quiet or silence/quiet produces shalom (though Isa 30:15 — quietness and trust = strength — suggests the latter). Gap deferred to Session D.

**T1.8.3 — Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?**

Yes — three major implications: (1) The shalom-as-covenant finding implicates R34 (covenant) directly — the covenant of peace (Isa 54:10, Eze 37:26) is both a peace term and a covenant term. Session D must examine whether the peace-covenant is a distinct covenant or the inner dimension of the Sinai/new covenant; (2) The shalom-as-trust-product finding implicates R163 (trust) — Isa 26:3 (trust → perfect peace) and Psa 4:8 (trust in God → lie down in peace) establish trust as peace's causal precondition. Session D must examine whether every instance of shalom-without-trust is either false peace (2509-002) or eschatological peace (2508-004); (3) The "Person IS peace" finding (Mic 5:5, Eph 2:14) implicates R130 (reconciliation) — Col 1:20 (making peace by the blood of the cross) and R130's apokatallassō vocabulary converge at the same event. The peace the Person IS and the reconciliation the cross achieves are the same event named from different angles.


## Anchor verses

The 117 anchor verses define this characteristic across its OWNER groups. Each anchor carries the full verse text, group context, and the Stage 2a analysis from `verse_context.analysis_note` — including the five cross-registry vision answers and any inline observations or SD pointers raised at this verse.


### G0425 (anesis) · group 429-001

*Term names relief from pressure or affliction — the inner-being experience of rest when burdens are lifted, or its absence when they persist*


**2Cor 2:13** — 2Cor 2:13 my spirit was not at rest because I did not find my brother Titus there. So I took leave of them and went on to Macedonia .


### G1514 (eirēneuō) · group 422-001

*Term names the inner disposition and active practice of relational peace — the will directed toward harmony with others*


**Rom 12:18** — Rom 12:18 If possible , so far as it depends on you , live peaceably with all .


### G1515 (eirēnē) · group 426-001

*Term names the inner-being peace given by God through Christ — the reconciled state before God and the interior experience of divine peace guarding the heart and mind*


**Joh 14:27** — Joh 14:27 Peace I leave with you ; my peace I give to you . Not as the world gives do I give to you . Let not your hearts be troubled , neither let them be afraid .


**Phili 4:7** — Phili 4:7 And the peace of God , which surpasses all understanding , will guard your hearts and your minds in Christ Jesus .


### G1515 (eirēnē) · group 426-002

*Term names peace as apostolic blessing and greeting — the invocation of divine shalom upon persons in the community of Christ*


**Rom 1:7** — Rom 1:7 To all those in Rome who are loved by God and called to be saints : Grace to you and peace from God our Father and the Lord Jesus Christ .


### G1515 (eirēnē) · group 426-003

*Term names peace as the eschatological and relational fruit of the kingdom — achieved by Christ, to be pursued in community and received as the Spirit's gift*


**Gal 5:22** — Gal 5:22 But the fruit of the Spirit is love , joy , peace , patience , kindness , goodness , faithfulness ,


**Eph 2:14** — Eph 2:14 For he himself is our peace , who has made us both one and has broken down in his flesh the dividing wall of hostility


### G1516 (eirēnikos) · group 427-001

*Term names peaceableness as an inner character quality — the disposition of the trained person or the wisdom from above, producing righteous fruit*


**Jam 3:17** — Jam 3:17 But the wisdom from above is first pure , then peaceable , gentle , open to reason , full of mercy and good fruits , impartial and sincere .


### G1517 (eirēnopoieō) · group 430-001

*Term names the act of making peace — Christ's reconciling work that restores the broken relationship between humanity and God*


**Col 1:20** — Col 1:20 and through him to reconcile to himself all things , whether on earth or in heaven , making peace by the blood of his cross .


### G2263 (ēremos) · group 428-001

*Term names peaceful quietude as an inner-being quality of life — the disposition of undisturbed, godly calm in social context*


**1Ti 2:2** — 1Ti 2:2 for kings and all who are in high positions , that we may lead a peaceful and quiet life , godly and dignified in every way.


### G2270 (hēsuchazō) · group 424-001

*Term names the inner disposition of settling into quiet — the ceasing of noise, argument, or resistance, whether in reverence, acceptance, or godly aspiration*

**Jer 30:10** — Jer 30:10 “Then fear not , O Jacob my servant , declares the Lord , nor be dismayed , O Israel ; for behold , I will save you from far away , and your offspring from the land of their captivity . Jacob shall return and have quiet and ease , and none shall make him afraid .


### H8253 (she.qet) · group 2583-001

*Term names quietness as a condition of divinely bestowed peace — the character of a reign and its people under God's gift of rest*


**1Ch 22:9** — 1Ch 22:9 Behold , a son shall be born to you who shall be a man of rest . I will give him rest from all his surrounding enemies . For his name shall be Solomon , and I will give peace and quiet to Israel in his days .


## Lexical foundation

**Vocabulary analysis (T7.1):**

**T7.1.1 — What are the primary Hebrew and Greek terms for this characteristic — and what do their root meanings reveal?**

Primary terms: (1) Hebrew: sha.lom (H7965G and sub-forms) — root SHA.L/SHA.L.M, meaning completeness, soundness, wholeness; from sha.lem (to be complete, to be at peace, to be paid in full); the root encodes the idea of nothing lacking, everything in its proper place. This is the most semantically significant root etymology in the registry — shalom is not "peace" in the modern reductive sense but "wholeness-completeness-settlement." The root's appearance in sha.lam (to pay, to make restitution, H7999) confirms: shalom is what exists when every debt is settled, every breach is healed, every deficiency is supplied. (2) Greek: eirēnē (G1515) — root uncertain, possibly from eirō (to join) — the condition of things joined together; in the LXX, eirēnē becomes the translation of shalom, absorbing its full semantic range. NT eirēnē is not a Greek concept Hellenised but a Hebrew concept translated. OBS-117-009, Q&A-013.

**T7.1.2 — What is the grammatical range of the primary term (noun, verb, adjective, participle) — and what does that range reveal about how the characteristic operates?**

Sha.lom (H7965G) functions as: (1) Noun (primary): the condition of peace — "in peace I will lie down" (Psa 4:8), "covenant of peace" (Isa 54:10); (2) Noun used as greeting formula: "Peace be to you" (1Sa 25:6, H7965K); (3) Noun functioning as an identity statement: "I am for peace" (Psa 120:7 — ani sha.lom, where sha.lom is the predicate nominative); (4) Noun used adverbially (H7965L): "wholly" (Jer 13:19 — all-verses-fail, no inner-being); (5) Noun in construct chain as descriptor: "covenant of peace" (brit sha.lom), "man of peace" (ish sha.lom, Psa 37:37), "Prince of Peace" (sar sha.lom, Isa 9:6). The identity-statement form (ani sha.lom — "I am peace") is analytically distinctive: sha.lom becomes a predicate nominative, identifying the person with the characteristic. This grammatical form is unique in the registry and is the most concentrated expression of the moral-character dimension.

**T7.1.3 — What is the semantic range of the primary term — across what breadth of meaning does it operate?**

Sha.lom's semantic range is the widest of any single term in the programme's vocabulary: (1) Completeness/soundness (Psa 38:3 — "no sha.lom in my bones"); (2) Safety/welfare (Jer 29:11 — "plans for sha.lom"); (3) Peace as inner condition (Psa 4:8 — "in sha.lom I will lie down"); (4) Peace as relational condition (Num 25:12 — "covenant of sha.lom"); (5) Peace as eschatological reality (Isa 9:7 — "no end to his sha.lom"); (6) Peace as messianic identity (Mic 5:5 — "he shall be their sha.lom"); (7) Welfare/prosperity (H7965I — prosperity of the wicked, Psa 73:3); (8) Greeting (H7965K — peace-blessing in greeting, 1Sa 25:6); (9) Friendship (H7965J — bond of close friendship, 1Ch 12:17); (10) Divine identity (H7965H — YHWH-Shalom, Judg 6:24). The range confirms: shalom is the most semantically integrative term in the registry.

**T7.1.4 — Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — disposition versus act, received versus given, condition versus quality?**

Yes — the vocabulary distinguishes: (1) State: sha.lom (H7965G — the condition), menuchah (H4496H — the resting state), shaqat (H8252 — the state of quiet); (2) Disposition/character: sha.lom (H7965G, group 2508-003 — moral orientation), hēsuchios (G2272 — gentle and quiet spirit as character), eirēnikos (G1516 — peaceableness as quality); (3) Act: shaqat (H8252 — to quiet, verbal form), eirēneuō (G1514 — to be at peace actively, volitional act), eirēnopoieō (G1517 — to make peace, the specific act of peacemaking); (4) Process/fruit: Gal 5:22 — peace as Spirit-produced fruit (the formative outcome). The vocabulary is unusually rich in distinguishing the state (what shalom is), the disposition (the orientation toward shalom), the act (making or pursuing shalom), and the fruit (peace formed over time).

**T7.1.5 — Does the vocabulary include a term for the structural opposite or absence of this characteristic?**

The vocabulary does not include a single dedicated antonym term, but several terms name peace's absence or corruption: (1) The "no sha.lom for the wicked" formula (Isa 48:22, 57:21) — sha.lom with negation particle; (2) The restlessness vocabulary: balah (to be disturbed/troubled), ra.ga (H7280A — to stir up/disturb) — these name the opposite condition; (3) Isa 57:20 — "like the tossing sea, for it cannot be quiet (sha.qat)" — inability to be quiet as the functional opposite of shaqat-peace; (4) The false-shalom pattern: the vocabulary distinguishes false sha.lom (the words of the false prophets, Jer 6:14) from genuine sha.lom, but the same word is used — the opposition is moral, not lexical. The absence of a dedicated antonym term is itself analytically significant: the biblical vocabulary defines peace positively (through its presence and character) rather than negatively (through a named opposite). Peace is not primarily defined by what it is not.

**T7.1.6 — Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?**

Yes — multiple person-type terms: (1) Ish sha.lom (Psa 37:37 — "man of peace") — the person whose character is peace; the construct chain ish + sha.lom makes peace the defining characteristic of the person; (2) Ani sha.lom (Psa 120:7 — "I am for peace/I am peace") — the most concentrated self-designation; sha.lom as personal identity not merely possession; (3) Anshei sha.lom (Jer 20:10, 38:22 — "men of sha.lom") — plural person-type term; men defined by their sha.lom-bond; used notably in the betrayal context; (4) G1516 eirēnikos — the peaceable person, one whose character is eirēnē (Jam 3:17, Heb 12:11); (5) Eirēnopoios (G1518 — "peacemaker," Mat 5:9) — owned by R103 but in the peace vocabulary's scope: the person whose characteristic act is making peace. The person-type vocabulary is rich: the registry defines not only the characteristic but the person whose nature is the characteristic.

**T7.1.7 — Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?**

Yes: (1) Psa 34:14 — "seek peace (sha.lom) and pursue it" — the imperative "seek" (baqash) applied to sha.lom; seeking-peace as a commanded act; (2) Eze 7:25 — "when anguish comes they will seek (baqash) peace, but there shall be none" — desperate seeking of sha.lom; (3) Jer 8:15, 14:19 — "we looked for peace" — qavah (to wait/hope for) applied to sha.lom; the seeking-of-peace-that-does-not-come; (4) Jer 29:7 — "seek (darash) the welfare" — darash (to seek/inquire) applied to the sha.lom of the city. The seeking-vocabulary is significant: sha.lom is not only given but sought — the data shows humans actively pursuing sha.lom, sometimes successfully (Psa 34:14 — commanded pursuit) and sometimes futilely (Eze 7:25 — sought but unavailable under judgment). The seeking of peace is itself theologically meaningful: it reflects the deep human orientation toward shalom even when it is unavailable.

**T7.1.8 — What does the LXX use of the vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?**

The LXX's consistent rendering of sha.lom with eirēnē is the foundational datum: the translation decision imports sha.lom's full semantic range into the Greek word, enabling the NT to use eirēnē with full shalom resonance. This is not a gap (the LXX-translation relationship is established and confirmed). However: the data flags NO_WORD_ANALYSIS for some terms, and LSJ (Greek lexicon) data is absent for the Greek terms in the readiness output — the §D lexical section showed no LSJ entries for the Greek eirēnē family. This means the LXX-development analysis is based on the translation relationship rather than full lexicographic comparison. Gap: a formal LXX analysis comparing sha.lom-eirēnē translation patterns (where sha.lom is not translated as eirēnē, and where eirēnē translates something other than sha.lom) would sharpen the boundary. This is beyond the current data's scope.

**T7.1.9 — Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?**

Yes — G1517 eirēnopoieō (to make peace, Col 1:20) is attested primarily in the NT context and represents a verbal neologism: combining eirēnē (peace) with poieō (to make/do) to create a term for the specific act of cosmic peace-making. This term is thin (1 active verse in the corpus, Col 1:20) but carries maximum theological weight — it names what Christ accomplished at the cross as a unique act. G1518 eirēnopoios (peacemaker, Mat 5:9) similarly compounds the root to create a person-type term. The NT coinage of these compound terms reflects the NT's need to describe both the act (eirēnopoieō) and the agent (eirēnopoios) of peace-making — concepts that the OT vocabulary named differently (sha.lom as noun, baqash sha.lom as seeking-peace). The new terminology reflects new theological content: the specific Christological act of making peace through the cross.

**T7.1.10 — What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?**

The full vocabulary arc — from SHA.L (root: completeness) through the shalom sub-forms (G/H/I/J/K/L) through the Hebrew silence/rest vocabulary (H5117/H5183A/H8252 etc.) through the Greek eirēnē family (G1515, G1514, G1516, G1517) — reveals that peace encompasses: (1) The root: completeness-as-ground; (2) The inner condition: wholeness, welfare, rest, quiet; (3) The relational form: friendship, greeting-blessing, covenant; (4) The divine identity: YHWH-Shalom, Prince of Peace; (5) The act: making peace (eirēnopoieō), being at peace (eirēneuō), pursuing peace (baqash sha.lom); (6) The person: man of peace, the peaceable (eirēnikos); (7) The eschatological telos: no end to his peace, eternal covenant. The arc confirms the T1 central finding (Q&A-033): shalom is a structured sequence (divine identity → covenant gift → inner condition → eschatological embodiment) whose vocabulary reflects every stage of that structure.


---

# Chapter 2 — How It Works

**Registry 117 — Peace** · *Word Impact Description*

Operations, faculties, constitutional location, relational axis, formative dimension. Drawn from T2 (Constitutional Location), T3 (Inner Faculties), T4 (Relational Interfaces), and T5 (Formative & Developmental).

---

## Constitutional location and boundaries

Where this characteristic operates within the spirit/soul/heart/mind/body structure.


### T2.1 — Spirit-Level Location

**Prompt 1: Is this characteristic explicitly located at the spirit level in the verse evidence?**

Partial evidence. The most direct instance: 2Cor 2:13 ("my spirit [pneuma] was not at rest [anesis] because I did not find my brother Titus there") — G0425 group 429-001 locates peace-as-relief directly in Paul's spirit. This is the clearest spirit-level location in the NT portion of the registry. In the OT, the spirit-level is less explicitly named for peace: ruach (spirit) does not appear as the explicit bearer of shalom in the anchor verse evidence. However, the Spirit's involvement is constitutive in the NT: Gal 5:22 (peace as Spirit-fruit, G1515 group 426-003) — peace is a product of the Spirit's presence, not explicitly located in the human spirit but produced by the divine Spirit within the person. The distinction matters: peace is produced by the Spirit but its experiential location in the data is primarily soul (nephesh) and heart (lev/kardia). Source: OBS-117-122 (prior session, Gal 5:22), Q&A-011.

**Prompt 2: Does the evidence indicate that this characteristic originates in or is primarily a spirit-level phenomenon?**

Spirit-level origin is supported in the NT but not dominant. Evidence: (1) Gal 5:22 — peace as Spirit-fruit means its origin is the divine Spirit working in the person; the human spirit is the reception point; (2) 1Th 5:23 ("the God of peace himself sanctify you completely; your whole spirit [pneuma] and soul [psuche] and body [soma] be kept blameless") — the God of peace addresses the whole person trichotomously, with spirit listed first; peace's sanctifying reach is total, covering the spirit level; (3) Rom 8:6 ("to set the mind on the Spirit is life and peace") — the Spirit-oriented mind produces peace, suggesting spirit-level orientation is the generator of inner peace. The evidence suggests peace originates at the spirit level (through the Spirit's presence) but is experienced throughout the person. Not primarily located in the spirit as its constitutional home — the heart and soul are the more explicit locations.

**Prompt 3: What does spirit-level location reveal about the nature and depth of this characteristic?**

The spirit-level data reveals that peace has its generative source in the deepest layer of the person — where the divine Spirit interacts with the human person (Gal 5:22, Rom 8:6). The significance: peace is not a surface emotional adjustment but a characteristic that involves the deepest interface between the human person and God. 2Cor 2:13 (spirit not at rest) shows that spirit-level restlessness is possible — the absence of peace penetrates to the spirit. 1Th 5:23 (sanctification of the whole person including spirit) shows that peace's reach is total and deep. The nature revealed: peace is a whole-person integrative condition whose deepest root is the spirit-level relationship with God, even though its most explicit anatomical location in the data is the soul and heart.


### T2.10 — Constitutional Movement

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic move across constitutional levels — from spirit to soul, from soul to body, or across boundaries in other directions?**

Yes — and this is the most analytically significant T2 finding for the peace registry. The constitutional movement of peace is extensively documented, operating in multiple directions: (1) Spirit → soul → body (downward cascade under loss of peace): The absence of peace radiates from the inner person outward: Deu 28:65 — peacelessness produces trembling heart (volitional/heart level) + failing eyes (body level) + languishing soul (soul level); Psa 32:3 — suppressed inner condition → bones wasting (body); Psa 38:3 — sin disrupting inner peace → no soundness in flesh, no health in bones. The movement under un-peace: inner disorder → somatic deterioration. (2) God → spirit → soul → body (downward cascade under the gift of peace): Gal 5:22 (Spirit produces peace as fruit) → Phili 4:7 (peace guards hearts and minds) → Psa 4:8 (peace enables the body to lie down and sleep). The gift moves from its divine source through the spirit/Spirit level into the heart and mind, and from there into somatic rest. (3) Body → inner (the redemptive reversal): Isa 53:5 — the Servant's wounded body → peace received in others' inner being; the movement against the normal direction, accomplished through the cross.

**Prompt 2: What does the evidence reveal about the sequence or pattern of that movement?**

Three patterns are identifiable: (1) Cascading loss pattern: inner peace → somatic peace is the normal condition; inner dis-peace → somatic deterioration follows the causal chain inner-to-body (Psa 32:3, 38:3, Deu 28:65). The sequence is consistent: first the inner person is disordered (by sin, by judgment, by absent trust), then the body registers the disorder. The body is the last to fail and the indicator of deep inner trouble. (2) Cascading gift pattern: God gives peace (Num 6:26, Joh 14:27) → heart and mind receive it (Phili 4:7, Isa 26:3) → body enacts it (Psa 4:8 — lying down in sleep). The sequence moves from divine source through inner-being faculties into somatic expression. The body's rest is the downstream expression of the inner peace received. (3) Redemptive reversal pattern: suffering body of the Servant (Isa 53:5) → inner peace produced in others; this reversal moves counter to the normal direction and is unique in the corpus. The three patterns together establish peace as a constitutionally mobile characteristic — it does not reside statically in one location but flows through the whole person, and flows in different directions under different conditions.


### T2.2 — Soul-Level Location

**Prompt 1: Is this characteristic identified in the verse evidence as a soul-level phenomenon?**

Yes — explicitly. The strongest evidence: (1) Lam 3:17 ("my soul [nephesh] is bereft of peace [shalom]; I have forgotten what happiness is") — nephesh is named as the bearer of peace's absence; OBS-117-144; (2) Psa 62:5 ("For God alone, O my soul [nephesh], wait in silence; for my hope is from him") — prior session, the soul commanded to silence itself before God; H4496H group 2585-001 — "the soul's own rest"; (3) Psa 131:2 ("I have calmed and quieted my soul [nephesh], like a weaned child with its mother; like a weaned child is my soul [nephesh] within me") — soul quieted, peace as the soul's condition; (4) Psa 55:18 ("He redeems my soul [nephesh] in safety [shalom] from the battle that I wage") — the soul directly named as the bearer of shalom-safety; OBS-117-149; (5) Jer 6:16 ("walk in it, and find rest for your souls [nephesh]") — rest/peace located explicitly in the soul. The soul is the most explicitly named inner-being location of peace in the data — more so than the spirit or heart in the Hebrew evidence.

**Prompt 2: What does soul-level location reveal about this characteristic's place in the innermost personal experience?**

Soul-level location establishes peace as a fundamental condition of the whole inner person's experience — not a cognitive state (mind-level) or a moral quality (character-level) but the experiential condition of the nephesh as living, feeling, wanting creature. The nephesh is the person as they are most fully themselves — most vulnerable, most alive, most in need. Locating shalom in the nephesh means: (1) When peace is present, the whole person flourishes (Psa 55:18 — soul in safety); (2) When peace is absent, the whole person is destitute (Lam 3:17 — soul bereft, happiness forgotten); (3) Peace can be commanded to the soul (Psa 62:5, 131:2) — the soul can be directed toward peace by addressing it as a subject. This last point is analytically significant: the soul is not passive in relation to peace but can be called to orientation. The soul-level location means peace is the deepest personal experiential reality — the condition in which the self-as-person rests or is bereft.


### T2.3 — Heart

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence locate this characteristic in the heart?**

Yes — with rich evidence. Key heart locations: (1) Deu 29:19 (2509-002 anchor, OBS-117-150) — "blesses himself in his heart [lev]" claiming shalom while walking in "stubbornness of my heart" — false peace is a heart-generated claim; the heart is the seat of the false shalom; (2) 1Ch 12:17 (2510-001 anchor, OBS-117-151) — "my heart will be joined to you" — the shalom of friendship is a heart-bond; (3) Joh 14:27 — "Let not your hearts [kardia] be troubled" — Christ's peace addresses the heart directly; the troubled heart and the peace are antithetical (G1515 426-001 anchor, prior session OBS-117-120); (4) Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God... will guard your hearts [kardia] and your minds" — the heart is the primary guarded territory; (5) Pro 12:20 — "those who plan peace have joy" — planning peace is a heart-act (prior session OBS-117-096 noted the heart-disposition of planning); (6) Psa 28:3 — "speak peace with their neighbors while evil is in their hearts" — heart as the locus of authentic or false orientation toward peace. The data shows 33 co-occurring verses with R183 (heart) — the highest co-occurrence count in the entire registry (G.2, OBS-117-016).

**Prompt 2: What does the heart-location reveal — what aspect of the heart's integrating function (knowing, willing, feeling, moral awareness) does this characteristic engage?**

The heart-location of peace engages all four of the heart's integrating functions: (1) Knowing (cognitive): Rom 8:6 ("to set the mind/heart on the Spirit is life and peace") — the heart's cognitive orientation toward God produces peace; the directed heart knows what produces peace; (2) Willing (volitional): Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace") — the heart's volitional orientation toward peace as identity; Isa 30:15 ("but you were unwilling") — the refusal of peace is a heart-act of will; (3) Feeling (affective): Joh 14:27 ("let not your hearts be troubled") — the troubled heart as the experiential opposite of peace; the heart as the seat of distress or rest; (4) Moral awareness (conscience): Deu 29:19 — the heart's stubbornness as the moral condition that generates false shalom; Psa 28:3 — evil in the heart contrasted with speaking peace. The heart is the integrating centre of peace's constitutional address: peace must be received, held, directed, and expressed from the heart. The heart-peace connection is the most comprehensive single-location engagement in the data.


### T2.4 — Mind

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence locate this characteristic in the mind?**

Yes — explicitly and centrally. The primary mind-location: Isa 26:3 ("You keep him in perfect peace whose mind [yetser — formed thought/disposition] is stayed on you, because he trusts in you") — OBS-117-144. This is the most direct mind-peace statement in the corpus: perfect peace (shalom shalom) is the condition of the mind that is directed toward and stayed upon God. The yetser is the formed inclination or disposition of the mind — its sustained directedness. In the NT: Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God... will guard your hearts and your minds [noema — thoughts, understanding]" — the mind is jointly named as the territory peace guards alongside the heart; Rom 8:6 — "to set the mind [phronema] on the Spirit is life and peace" — the mind's orientation determines whether peace is present. The mind is the second major constitutional location of peace after the soul, and the most explicitly causal: directing the mind toward God produces peace; directing the mind elsewhere forfeits it.

**Prompt 2: What does mind-location reveal — what aspect of the mind's function (thought, discernment, understanding) does this characteristic engage?**

The mind-peace connection primarily engages the mind's directional function — the capacity of the mind to fix itself upon an object. Isa 26:3 (yetser) — the "stayed" or "fixed" mind: peace requires the mind to be anchored, not drifting. The mental act that produces peace is not thought-content (what the mind thinks about) but mental orientation (what the mind is stayed upon). Rom 8:6 (phronema — the mind's settled disposition/inclination) — the mind's habitual orientation toward the Spirit produces peace; toward the flesh produces death. Phili 4:7 (noema — the products of thought, understanding) — the mind's thoughts are what peace guards: peace stands sentinel over the mind's outputs. Three aspects: (1) Orientation (Isa 26:3) — where the mind is directed; (2) Disposition (Rom 8:6) — what the mind is habitually inclined toward; (3) Output (Phili 4:7) — the contents of thought that peace protects. The mind's peace is a total engagement — orientation, disposition, and output are all in view.


### T2.5 — Other Soul Subsets

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence surface any soul-level location beyond heart and mind for this characteristic?**

Yes — two additional soul-subset locations appear: (1) The inward parts / belly (meah — inward parts, bowels): Job 30:27 ("My inward parts [meah] are in turmoil and never still") — OBS-117-066 from prior session; the deepest visceral inner location of restlessness; Jer 4:19 ("My anguish, my anguish! I writhe in pain! Oh the walls of my heart! My heart is beating wildly; I cannot keep silent") — the peace-vocabulary's absence expressed through the visceral organs; (2) The inner person / hidden person of the heart: 1Pe 3:4 ("the hidden person [kryptos anthrōpos] of the heart with the imperishable beauty of a gentle and quiet spirit [pneuma]") — G2272 group 2559-001; this is the NT's most explicit inner-being anatomy for peace — the hidden person of the heart whose character is the gentle-quiet spirit. The hidden person is not heart or spirit alone but the person-as-inner-being in its most comprehensive sense. OBS-117-128 from prior session.

**Prompt 2: If so, what is that location, and what does it reveal?**

(1) Inward parts/belly (meah): locates peace-restlessness at the level of the deepest visceral experience — the gut-level inner experience. This is not a cognitive or volitional location but a felt-body-interior location. Its significance: restlessness and peace penetrate below the level of deliberate consciousness to the visceral core of the person. This deepens the somatic evidence (T2.6 below). (2) Hidden person of the heart (1Pe 3:4): this is the most theologically rich additional location. The "hidden person" is the inner self in its deepest reality — the person as God sees them, not as they appear outwardly. The quality of this hidden person is the gentle-quiet spirit. The revelation: the ultimate location of peace as character is not the publicly visible person but the hidden inner person — the one precious to God (1Pe 3:4: "which in God's sight is very precious"). Peace at this level is not performance but being. The hidden-person location connects to the T1 finding about the formed character of peace (Q&A-029) — the peace that is formed becomes the hidden person's defining quality.


### T2.6 — Body — Significance

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence link this characteristic to a specific body part?**

Yes — and this is one of the most extensive somatic evidence sets in the programme. Multiple body parts explicitly linked to peace's presence or absence: (1) Bones (etsem): Psa 32:3 ("my bones wasted away through my groaning all day long") — withheld confession/peace produces bone deterioration; Psa 38:3 ("there is no health [shalom] in my bones because of my sin") — OBS-117-149; shalom named as the soundness-of-bones; (2) Flesh (basar): Psa 38:3 ("there is no soundness in my flesh because of your indignation") — flesh linked to peace-as-soundness; (3) Eyes: Deu 28:65 ("failing eyes") — peacelessness in judgment produces ocular failure; (4) Feet/sole: Deu 28:65 ("no resting place for the sole of your foot") — the body's connection to the land as peace's somatic expression; (5) Inward parts/belly (meah): Job 30:27 — OBS-117-066; (6) Body-sleep (Psa 4:8) — the whole body at rest as the expression of peace ("I will both lie down and sleep"); (7) Wounds (Isa 53:5 — "with his wounds we are healed") — the Servant's body as the instrument of peace's purchase.

**Prompt 2: If so, what is Scripture doing by making that link — is it emphatic, functional, expressive, indicative, or mediating?**

The body-part links serve multiple functions: (1) Indicative — the body's condition indicates the inner-being condition: Psa 38:3 (no soundness in flesh/no health in bones because of sin) — the body reads out the inner state of peacelessness; the body is a diagnostic display of the inner condition; (2) Emphatic — Deu 28:65 accumulates three somatic signals (trembling heart, failing eyes, languishing soul) to intensify the portrait of peacelessness in judgment; the body amplifies the inner condition; (3) Expressive — Psa 4:8 (lying down and sleeping) — the body enacts peace by doing what peace enables; sleep is the body's expression of inner security; (4) Mediating — Isa 53:5 ("with his wounds we are healed") — the Servant's body mediates peace to others; the physical wounds are the mechanism of the relational restoration. The most significant body-link is Psa 38:3: the explicit naming of shalom as soundness-of-bones under sin's influence establishes that inner peace and somatic integrity are structurally connected — not metaphorically but causally.


### T2.7 — Body — Direction

**Prompt 1: Where a body-characteristic link exists, which direction does it run — does the soul express through the body, does the body feed back to the soul, or does it run in both directions?**

Both directions are evidenced, with the dominant direction being inner-state → body: (1) Inner → body (primary direction): Psa 38:3 — sin disrupts inner peace → no health in bones; Psa 32:3 — suppressed confession (inner withholding) → bones wasting; Deu 28:65 — peacelessness in judgment → trembling heart, failing eyes, languishing soul. The inner condition of un-peace expresses itself through somatic deterioration. (2) Body → inner: Isa 53:5 ("with his wounds we are healed") — the Servant's somatic wounds produce inner healing/peace for others; the body of the Servant becomes the mechanism of inner restoration for the many. This is the most theologically significant body→inner direction in the corpus — a body bearing suffering that produces inner peace in others. (3) Body expressing inner: Psa 4:8 — the body lying down and sleeping expresses an already-present inner peace; the body does not produce peace but enacts it. The primary causal direction is inner → body; the Isa 53:5 direction (body → inner) is the unique redemptive reversal.

**Prompt 2: What is the consequence of that directionality for understanding the characteristic?**

The primary direction (inner → body) establishes peace as a genuinely somatic characteristic — its absence or presence registers in the body because the body is the outward expression of the inner state. This has two consequences: (1) Peace cannot be fully present in the inner person while the body is in somatic distress caused by inner disorder — the body is a truth-telling indicator of the inner condition (Psa 38:3, 32:3); a person who claims inner peace while their body deteriorates under the weight of unconfessed sin is in the false-shalom pattern; (2) The redemptive reversal (Isa 53:5 body → inner) is extraordinary precisely because it runs against the normal causal direction: normally inner disorder produces somatic deterioration; in the Servant's case, somatic suffering produces inner healing in others. The directionality finding deepens the T1 paradox (Q&A-023): peace comes through its opposite conditions not only experientially but physically.


### T2.8 — Body — Deposit

**Prompt 1: Does sustained operation of this characteristic leave a constitutional deposit in the body or its design — including DNA or generational consequence?**

The evidence provides indirect but not conclusive evidence for a bodily deposit. What is present: (1) Psa 38:3 and 32:3 establish that sustained absence of peace (inner disorder) produces somatic deterioration — bones wasting, no soundness in flesh. This implies that sustained presence of peace should produce the inverse — somatic health and soundness; (2) Pro 3:2 ("length of days and years of life and peace they will add to you") — wisdom-and-peace produces length of life; shalom is associated with longevity as its somatic fruit; (3) Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace") — wisdom's characteristic product is peace as a sustained life-condition. The wisdom literature implies that sustained shalom has somatic extension effects (length of life). However: the data does not explicitly address constitutional deposit in the technical sense (DNA, generational transmission). The silence is methodological rather than substantive — the biblical vocabulary does not operate with the category of genetic or physiological deposit. Gap: the question of generational or developmental deposit for sustained peace is not addressable from this data. This finding feeds directly into T5.7.

**Prompt 2: What evidence supports or contradicts this?**

Supporting: Pro 3:2 (peace associated with longer life — a life-outcome that implies somatic extension); the somatic-deterioration pattern under sustained peacelessness (Psa 38:3, 32:3, Deu 28:65) implies by negation that sustained peace protects somatic integrity. Contradicting: no verse explicitly claims that shalom leaves a genetic or physiological deposit. The closest is Isa 66:12 ("like a river... like an overflowing stream") — the image of peace as overflowing suggests abundance rather than deposit. Neutral: 1Th 5:23 (the God of peace sanctifying the whole person — spirit, soul, body) suggests peace's reach is total but does not specify deposit mechanisms. The evidence supports somatic consequence of peace (in both directions) but not constitutional deposit in the genetic/biological sense. Gap explicitly noted for T5.7.

**Prompt 3: If the evidence is silent, note this explicitly. This finding feeds directly into T5.7.**

The evidence is partially silent on constitutional deposit in the technical sense. The data establishes somatic consequence (in both directions) but not deposit. T5.7 consequence: since the evidence for a constitutional body deposit is indirect and inconclusive, T5.7 will not be able to affirm a deposit finding — all three T5.7 prompts will receive N (not applicable) unless T5 analysis surfaces new evidence. This is recorded here for that dependency. The somatic consequence findings (T2.6, T2.7) remain valid independent of the deposit question.


### T2.9 — Origin and Source

**Prompt 1: Where does this characteristic originate constitutionally — is it generated from within the person, received from outside, bestowed by God, or carried generationally?**

The data establishes a primary origin and two secondary origins: (1) Primary — bestowed by God: the overwhelming weight of the evidence shows shalom as divine gift. The Aaronic blessing (Num 6:26 — "the Lord... give you peace"), God's covenant of peace (Isa 54:10), God speaking peace to his saints (Psa 85:8), God planning welfare (Jer 29:11), Christ as the peace-giver (Joh 14:27 — "my peace I give to you"), Spirit-fruit peace (Gal 5:22) — all establish God as the constitutional source. The human person cannot generate authentic shalom from within. (2) Secondary — formed from within through orientation: Isa 26:3 — the mind stayed on God produces perfect peace; the person's own cognitive act of orientation enables the reception of what God provides. This is not generation from within but reception through orientation; (3) Generated falsely from within: the false-shalom pattern (Deu 29:19, Jer 6:14) — the person can generate a counterfeit inner condition of peace by self-deception or false claim, but this is precisely what the data condemns. Authentic constitutional origin: divine gift received through trust and covenant orientation.

**Prompt 2: What does the evidence reveal about whether the origin is singular or multiple?**

The origin is singular in ground but multiple in mechanism. Ground: God alone is the ultimate source — whether through covenant (2508-002), through Christ (426-001, Eph 2:14), or through the Spirit (Gal 5:22), all peace traces back to God as its constitutional source. Mechanism: three distinct modes of origination appear in the data: (1) Covenantal gift — God gives shalom through covenant (Num 6:26, Isa 54:10); the covenant is the structural channel; (2) Christological accomplishment — peace purchased through the cross (Isa 53:5, Col 1:20, G1517 430-001); Christ's act constitutes the peace; (3) Pneumatological formation — peace formed as Spirit-fruit (Gal 5:22); the Spirit produces peace over time within the person. These three mechanisms correspond to the three persons of the Trinity — the origin is Trinitarian in structure, though the data does not use that language explicitly. The singular ground (God) and multiple mechanisms prevent both monism (peace from only one channel) and pluralism (peace from multiple independent sources).


## Inner faculties engaged

How this characteristic engages the eleven inner-being faculties: perception, cognition, memory, affect, creativity, volition, agency, moral evaluation, conscience, conscientiousness, and relational capacity.


### T3.1 — Perception

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic engage the perceptive faculty — the inner senses including hearing, sight, taste, touch, smell, and spiritual discernment — and if so, which inner sense and how?**

Yes — peace engages perception primarily in its negative form: the perceptive faculty is disturbed, dulled, or altered when peace is absent. Evidence: (1) Deu 28:65 — "failing eyes" as a somatic expression of peacelessness; the eyes (a perceptive organ) fail under the condition of un-peace in judgment; (2) The silence vocabulary in this registry (H1827 demamah, G4602 sigē) names the threshold-quiet before perception of the divine voice — 1Ki 19:12 (still small voice after the demamah-silence), OBS-117-069 from prior session; the silence that is peace's companion is precisely the condition that enables divine perception; (3) Psa 131:2 — "I have calmed and quieted my soul... like a weaned child" — the quieted soul is positioned for receptive perception; restlessness prevents this.

**Prompt 2: Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair the perceptive faculty in the person?**

Peace enables perception — specifically, the silence/stillness dimension of peace enables the perception of the divine voice and divine reality. The pattern: demamah (profound silence, H1827) is the threshold before divine speech (1Ki 19:12, Job 4:16 — prior session OBS-117-069, 070); the quieted soul is receptive; the restless soul cannot hear what silence carries. Conversely, the absence of peace impairs perception — "failing eyes" (Deu 28:65) is the somatic expression. The mechanism: peace as stillness creates the condition for perception; restlessness as noise prevents it. This is the hearing-direction of the silence vocabulary: peace enables the person to be still enough to perceive God.

**Prompt 3: What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with perception reveal about the nature of this characteristic?**

The perception engagement reveals that peace is not only a passive condition (a state received) but an enabling posture — the person at peace is constitutionally equipped to perceive things they cannot hear when restless. The demamah threshold (1Ki 19:12) is analytically significant: the divine voice comes not in wind, earthquake, or fire (the dramatic) but in the still small voice — and only the person who has moved through silence can hear it. This means peace has a gnomic/perceptual consequence: the peace-bearing person perceives differently. Peace as the enabling condition for divine perception is a new finding.


### T3.10 — Conscientiousness

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic engage conscientiousness — the integrated response of moral awareness, volition, and action — and if so, how?**

Yes. Conscientiousness as the integration of moral awareness, volition, and action is precisely what the peace-as-character data describes. Evidence: (1) Mal 2:6 — Levi "walked with me in peace and uprightness, and turned many from iniquity" — the three elements: moral awareness (uprightness), volitional commitment (walking with God), and consequential action (turning many from iniquity) are all present in the portrait of the peaceable priest; (2) Jer 29:7 — "seek the welfare of the city... pray to the Lord on its behalf, for in its welfare you will find your welfare" — conscience (awareness of responsibility), volition (seek, pray), and action (community engagement) integrated in the peace-orientation; (3) H2790B group 408-001 — holding silence under emotional provocation — the conscientious restraint of impulse in the service of peace. Conscientiousness is the enacted form of the peace-as-moral-character dimension.

**Prompt 2: Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair conscientiousness in the person?**

Authentic peace deepens conscientiousness; false peace impairs it. (1) Deepens: the person who is genuinely at peace is in a stable inner condition from which conscientious action flows — Mal 2:6 (walked in peace → turned many from iniquity); the stable inner orientation of peace produces consistent moral response; (2) Impairs: complacent ease (H7600 group 413-001, Amo 6:1 — "woe to those at ease in Zion") represents peace's corruption as conscientiousness-impairment — the at-ease person fails to respond to what conscience and moral awareness demand; false shalom produces precisely the opposite of conscientiousness: comfort without response. The distinction between authentic shalom and complacent ease is most sharply felt here: one deepens conscientiousness, the other destroys it.

**Prompt 3: What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with conscientiousness reveal about the nature of this characteristic?**

The conscientiousness pattern reveals that authentic peace is the inner condition that enables sustained moral-volitional-active integrity — the person at genuine peace is the person who can maintain consistent moral awareness, make the right volitional choices, and act on them over time. The portrait of Levi (Mal 2:6) is the paradigm case: the peaceful person is the most consistently conscientious person, not the least. This directly answers the potential misunderstanding that peace means passivity or disengagement — the data establishes the opposite. Peace is the enabling condition for the most integrated form of moral-volitional-active response.


### T3.11 — Relational Capacity

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic engage the relational capacity — the constitutional equipment for genuine connection with another person — and if so, how?**

Yes — centrally. Peace is among the most relationally engaged characteristics in the programme. Evidence spans multiple dimensions: (1) Friendship bond (H7965J, 2510-001) — shalom as the inner relational bond of loyalty and heart-union (1Ch 12:17); (2) Greeting-blessing (H7965K, 2511-001) — the invocation of peace as the act of goodwill toward another; (3) Covenant peace (2508-002) — the relational condition between God and his people; (4) Peacemaking (G1517, Jam 3:18) — the capacity to make peace between parties; (5) G1514 eirēneuō — actively being at peace with all (Rom 12:18); (6) Eph 2:14-16 — Christ making peace between Jew and Gentile, creating one body. The relational dimension of peace is not peripheral but constitutive — the peace vocabulary is inherently directed toward-another.

**Prompt 2: Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair relational capacity in the person?**

Authentic peace deeply enables and deepens relational capacity. Evidence: (1) 1Ch 12:17 — the sha.lom-friendship produces heart-union; (2) Eph 2:14-16 — Christ's peace breaks down the "dividing wall of hostility" and creates one body from two peoples — peace is the engine of the most dramatic relational restoration in the NT; (3) Jer 29:7 — seeking the welfare of the exile city requires expanding relational capacity to include the former enemy's community; (4) 1Sa 25:6 (2511-001) — the greeting-blessing as an act of relational goodwill. Conversely: peacelessness impairs relational capacity — Gen 37:4 ("they hated him and could not speak peacefully to him") — the absence of sha.lom-orientation makes genuine relational connection impossible; Jer 20:10 (anshei sha.lom who betray) — false sha.lom destroys relational capacity through betrayal. The instruction note for T3.11 distinguishes characteristics that build/sustain connection from those that restore/repair it — this registry does both: shalom builds connection (friendship, greeting, covenant) and restores broken connection (Eph 2:14).

**Prompt 3: What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with relational capacity reveal about the nature of this characteristic?**

The relational pattern reveals that peace is constitutionally relational — it cannot be fully described as an inner private condition without reference to the other toward whom it is directed. The "I am for peace" (Psa 120:7) is not merely an inner statement but a relational declaration against the other party's war-orientation. The covenant of peace (2508-002) is inherently two-party. The friendship bond (2510-001) is inherently toward-another. The peace that guards the heart (Phili 4:7) is received in the context of prayer and petition to God. The conclusion: peace is both inner and relational simultaneously — the inner condition and the relational orientation are not separable dimensions but two faces of the same reality. The interiority and the relationality of peace are co-constitutive.


### T3.2 — Cognition

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic engage the cognitive faculty — knowing, understanding, discerning — and if so, how?**

Yes — centrally. The cognitive engagement of peace is established in T2.4: Isa 26:3 (yetser — the formed mental disposition/thought, "whose mind is stayed on you"), Rom 8:6 (phronema — mind's settled orientation), Phili 4:7 (noema — thoughts guarded by peace). The cognitive faculty is both the condition for peace (mind stayed on God → peace) and the territory peace guards (peace guards thoughts/minds). Two-directional engagement: cognition produces peace when rightly directed; peace guards cognition when received.

**Prompt 2: Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair cognition in the person?**

Peace enables and deepens cognition. Evidence: (1) Isa 32:17 — "the effect of righteousness will be peace, and the result of righteousness, quietness and trust forever" — the sustained peace-condition produces trust which is the basis for deeper knowing; (2) Ecc 9:17 — "the words of the wise heard in quiet [nachat] are better than the shouting of a ruler among fools" — the quiet condition enables wisdom reception (prior session OBS-117-100 context); (3) The wisdom-peace correlation: Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace") — wisdom's paths produce peace; peace and wisdom are mutually enabling. Conversely: the restless inner state ("cannot be quiet," Isa 57:20 — prior session OBS-117-114 context) produces "mire and dirt" — cognitive disorder and moral opacity. Peace is cognitively enabling; restlessness impairs.

**Prompt 3: What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with cognition reveal about the nature of this characteristic?**

The cognition pattern establishes a reciprocal relationship: right cognition (mind stayed on God) generates peace; peace in turn enables deeper cognition (wisdom reception, trust-grounded knowing). This is not a simple linear chain but a reinforcing loop — the peaceful mind perceives more, knows more, trusts more, and thereby receives more peace. The implication for Session D: R100 (knowledge) and R163 (trust) are structurally connected to peace through this cognitive loop.


### T3.3 — Memory

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic engage the memory faculty — the holding and retrieving of inner-being reality across time — and if so, how?**

Yes — in its negative form most directly. Lam 3:17 ("my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is") — peace-deprivation produces the loss of remembered happiness. The person bereft of shalom cannot recall what flourishing was like — memory of wellbeing is inaccessible in the peaceless condition. This is a striking data point: peace's absence disrupts not only the present experience but the person's ability to remember peace. Conversely: Isa 38:17 ("it was for my welfare that I had great bitterness") — Hezekiah's retrospective memory of peace-through-suffering suggests that the person who has received peace carries a memory of its arrival that shapes their understanding of God.

**Prompt 2: Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair memory in the person?**

Peace-deprivation impairs memory (Lam 3:17 — forgotten happiness); peace-restoration enables retrospective memory of God's faithfulness. The Lam 3 context is particularly rich: the movement from Lam 3:17 ("forgotten") to Lam 3:21-22 ("but this I call to mind, and therefore I have hope: the steadfast love of the Lord never ceases") — the act of remembering God's hesed breaks through the peaceless condition and restores hope. The memory of God's covenant faithfulness is the cognitive act that enables the return to peace. Peace and memory have a mutual relationship: peace enables the memory of grace; the memory of grace enables the recovery of peace.

**Prompt 3: What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with memory reveal about the nature of this characteristic?**

The memory engagement reveals that peace has a temporal dimension — it exists in time, can be remembered, can be forgotten, and can be recovered through the act of remembering God's faithfulness. The Lam 3 pattern (forgotten → remembered → hope) shows that the memory of shalom is recoverable through deliberate recollection of covenant faithfulness, even from the depth of peacelessness. This connects to the T5 formation question: peace is not only a present condition but a remembered and hoped-for reality — it has a narrative arc in the person's experience.


### T3.4 — Affect
